<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<p><b>Plants</b> are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdom</a> <b>Plantae</b>. Historically, the plant kingdom encompassed all living things that were not <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, and included <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>; however, all current definitions of Plantae exclude the fungi and some algae, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>). By one definition, plants form the <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> <a href="/wiki/Viridiplantae" title="Viridiplantae">Viridiplantae</a> (Latin name for "green plants") which is sister of the <a href="/wiki/Glaucophyte" title="Glaucophyte">Glaucophyta</a>, and consists of the <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Embryophyte" title="Embryophyte">Embryophyta</a> (land plants). The latter includes the <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">ferns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fern_ally" title="Fern ally">their allies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hornwort" title="Hornwort">hornworts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liverworts" title="Liverworts">liverworts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>.
</p><p>Most plants are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> organisms. Green plants obtain most of their energy from <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> via <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by primary <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a> that are derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiosis" title="Endosymbiosis">endosymbiosis</a> with <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>. Their chloroplasts contain <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophylls</a> a and b, which gives them their green color. Some plants are <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_plant" title="Parasitic plant">parasitic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mycotroph" title="Mycotroph">mycotrophic</a> and have lost the ability to produce normal amounts of chlorophyll or to photosynthesize, but still have flowers, fruits, and seeds. Plants are characterized by <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alternation_of_generations" title="Alternation of generations">alternation of generations</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual reproduction</a> is also common.
</p><p>There are about 320,000 known <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of plants, of which the great majority, some 260–290 thousand, <a href="/wiki/Spermatophyte" title="Spermatophyte">produce seeds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNdata_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNdata-5">[5]</a></sup> Green plants provide a substantial proportion of the world's molecular oxygen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behrenfeld_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-behrenfeld-6">[6]</a></sup> and are the basis of most of Earth's ecosystems. Plants that produce <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a> also form basic human foods and have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domesticated" title="Domesticated">domesticated</a> for millennia. Plants have many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants_in_culture" title="Plants in culture">cultural</a> and other uses, as ornaments, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Building_materials" title="Building materials">building materials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Writing_material" title="Writing material">writing material</a> and, in great variety, they have been the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicinal_plant" title="Medicinal plant">source of medicines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_drug" title="Psychoactive drug">psychoactive drugs</a>. The scientific study of plants is known as <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a>, a branch of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>
<p>All living things were traditionally placed into one of two groups, plants and animals. This classification may date from <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384 BC – 322 BC), who made the distinction between plants, which generally do not move, and animals, which often are mobile to catch their food. Much later, when <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> (1707–1778) created the basis of the modern system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_classification" title="Scientific classification">scientific classification</a>, these two groups became the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdoms</a> Vegetabilia (later Metaphyta or Plantae) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animalia" title="Animalia">Animalia</a> (also called Metazoa). Since then, it has become clear that the plant kingdom as originally defined included several unrelated groups, and the <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> and several groups of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> were removed to new kingdoms. However, these organisms are still sometimes considered plants, particularly in informal contexts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The term "plant" generally implies the possession of the following traits: multicellularity, possession of cell walls containing <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, and the ability to carry out photosynthesis with primary chloroplasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlplant[2]_–_Definition_from_the_Merriam-Webster_Online_Dictionary_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlplant[2]_–_Definition_from_the_Merriam-Webster_Online_Dictionary-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlplant_(life_form)_--_Britannica_Online_Encyclopedia_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlplant_(life_form)_--_Britannica_Online_Encyclopedia-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_definitions_of_Plantae">Current definitions of Plantae<span class="anchor" id="Current_definitions_of_Plantae"></span></span></h3>
<p>When the name Plantae or plant is applied to a specific group of organisms or <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a>, it usually refers to one of four concepts. From least to most inclusive, these four groupings are:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Name(s)
</th>
<th>Scope
</th>
<th>Description
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Land plants, also known as <a href="/wiki/Embryophyte" title="Embryophyte">Embryophyta</a>
</td>
<td>Plantae <i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#sensu_strictissimo" title="Glossary of botanical terms">sensu strictissimo</a></i>
</td>
<td><b>Plants in the strictest sense</b> include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liverworts" title="Liverworts">liverworts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hornworts" title="Hornworts">hornworts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosses" title="Mosses">mosses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a>, as well as fossil plants similar to these surviving groups (e.g., Metaphyta <small>Whittaker, 1969</small>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ib.usp.br_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ib.usp.br-9">[9]</a></sup> Plantae <small><a href="/wiki/Lynn_Margulis" title="Lynn Margulis">Margulis</a>, 1971</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>).
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Green plants</b>, also known as <b><a href="/wiki/Viridiplantae" title="Viridiplantae">Viridiplantae</a></b>, <b>Viridiphyta</b>, <b>Chlorobionta</b> or <b>Chloroplastida</b>
</td>
<td>Plantae <i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#sensu_stricto" title="Glossary of botanical terms">sensu stricto</a></i>
</td>
<td><b>Plants in a strict sense</b> include the <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a>, and land plants that emerged within them, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stonewort" title="Stonewort">stoneworts</a>. The relationships between plant groups are still being worked out, and the names given to them vary considerably. The <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> Viridiplantae encompasses a group of organisms that have <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a>, possess <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophylls</a> <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> and have <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">plastids</a> bound by only two membranes that are capable of photosynthesis and of storing starch. This clade is the main subject of this article (e.g., Plantae <small><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Copeland" title="Herbert Copeland">Copeland</a>, 1956</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>).
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Archaeplastida" title="Archaeplastida">Archaeplastida</a>, also known as Plastida or Primoplantae
</td>
<td>Plantae <i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#sensu_lato" title="Glossary of botanical terms">sensu lato</a></i>
</td>
<td><b>Plants in a broad sense</b> comprise the green plants listed above plus the red algae (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhodophyta" title="Rhodophyta">Rhodophyta</a>) and the glaucophyte algae (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaucophyta" title="Glaucophyta">Glaucophyta</a>) that store <a href="/wiki/Floridean_starch" title="Floridean starch">Floridean starch</a> outside the plastids, in the cytoplasm. This clade includes all of the organisms that eons ago acquired their <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast#Primary_endosymbiosis" title="Chloroplast">primary chloroplasts</a> directly by engulfing <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> (e.g., Plantae <small>Cavalier-Smith, 1981</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>).
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/List_of_systems_of_plant_taxonomy" title="List of systems of plant taxonomy">Old definitions of plant</a> (obsolete)
</td>
<td>Plantae <i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#sensu_amplo" title="Glossary of botanical terms">sensu amplo</a></i>
</td>
<td><b>Plants in the widest sense</b> refers to older, obsolete classifications that placed diverse algae, fungi or bacteria in Plantae (e.g., Plantae or Vegetabilia <small>Linnaeus</small>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Plantae <small>Haeckel 1866</small>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Metaphyta <small>Haeckel, 1894</small>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Plantae <small>Whittaker, 1969</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ib.usp.br_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-ib.usp.br-9">[9]</a></sup>).
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Another way of looking at the relationships between the different groups that have been called "plants" is through a <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a>, which shows their evolutionary relationships. These are not yet completely settled, but <span class="cleanup-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">one accepted relationship between the three groups described above is shown below</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="which of the references has exactly this cladogram? (November 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sánchez-Baracaldo_E7737–E7745_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sánchez-Baracaldo_E7737–E7745-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gitzendanner_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gitzendanner-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (September 2022)">excessive citations</span></a></i>]</sup> Those which have been called "plants" are in bold (some minor groups have been omitted).
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table><p>The way in which the groups of green algae are combined and named varies considerably between authors.
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algae">Algae</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3308" data-file-width="2346" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg/220px-Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg/330px-Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg/440px-Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haeckel_Siphoneae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">Green algae</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Kunstformen_der_Natur" title="Kunstformen der Natur">Kunstformen der Natur</a></i>, 1904.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">Algae</a></div>
<p>Algae consist of several groups of organisms which produce food by photosynthesis and thus have traditionally been included in the plant kingdom. The <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweeds</a> range from large multicellular algae to single-celled organisms and are classified into three groups, the <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_algae" title="Red algae">red algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brown_algae" title="Brown algae">brown algae</a>. There is good evidence that the brown algae evolved independently from the others, from non-photosynthetic ancestors that formed endosymbiotic relationships with red algae rather than from cyanobacteria, and they are no longer classified as plants as defined here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven_2005_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raven_2005-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Viridiplantae, the green plants – green algae and land plants – form a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>, a group consisting of all the descendants of a common ancestor. With a few exceptions, the green plants have the following features in common; primary <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a> derived from cyanobacteria containing <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophylls</a> <i>a</i> and <i>b</i>, cell walls containing <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, and food stores in the form of <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> contained within the plastids. They undergo closed <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> without <a href="/wiki/Centriole" title="Centriole">centrioles</a>, and typically have <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a> with flat cristae. The <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a> of green plants are surrounded by two membranes, suggesting they originated directly from endosymbiotic <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>.
</p><p>Two additional groups, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhodophyta" title="Rhodophyta">Rhodophyta</a> (red algae) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaucophyta" title="Glaucophyta">Glaucophyta</a> (glaucophyte algae), also have primary chloroplasts that appear to be derived directly from endosymbiotic <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, although they differ from Viridiplantae in the pigments which are used in photosynthesis and so are different in colour. These groups also differ from green plants in that the storage polysaccharide is <a href="/wiki/Floridean_starch" title="Floridean starch">floridean starch</a> and is stored in the cytoplasm rather than in the plastids. They appear to have had a common origin with Viridiplantae and the three groups form the clade <a href="/wiki/Archaeplastida" title="Archaeplastida">Archaeplastida</a>, whose name implies that their chloroplasts were derived from a single ancient endosymbiotic event. This is the broadest modern definition of the term 'plant'.
</p><p>In contrast, most other algae (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Heterokont" title="Heterokont">brown algae/diatoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haptophyte" title="Haptophyte">haptophytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dinoflagellate" title="Dinoflagellate">dinoflagellates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Euglenid" title="Euglenid">euglenids</a>) not only have different pigments but also have chloroplasts with three or four surrounding membranes. They are not close relatives of the Archaeplastida, presumably having acquired chloroplasts separately from ingested or symbiotic green and red algae. They are thus not included in even the broadest modern definition of the plant kingdom, although they were in the past.
</p><p>The green plants or Viridiplantae were traditionally divided into the green algae (including the stoneworts) and the land plants. However, it is now known that the land plants evolved from within a group of green algae, so that the green algae by themselves are a <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a> group, i.e. a group that excludes some of the descendants of a common ancestor. Paraphyletic groups are generally avoided in modern classifications, so that in recent treatments the Viridiplantae have been divided into two clades, the <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyta" title="Chlorophyta">Chlorophyta</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Streptophyta" title="Streptophyta">Streptophyta</a> (including the land plants and Charophyta).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LewisMcCourt2004_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-LewisMcCourt2004-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckerMarin2009_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-BeckerMarin2009-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chlorophyta (a name that has also been used for <i>all</i> green algae) are the sister group to the Charophytes, from which the land plants evolved. There are about 4,300 species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algaebase_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algaebase-29">[29]</a></sup> mainly unicellular or multicellular marine organisms such as the sea lettuce, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulva_(genus)" title="Ulva (genus)">Ulva</a></i>.
</p><p>The other group within the Viridiplantae are the mainly freshwater or terrestrial Streptophyta, which consists of the land plants together with the Charophyta, itself consisting of several groups of green algae such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desmid" title="Desmid">desmids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charales" title="Charales">stoneworts</a>. Streptophyte algae are either unicellular or form multicellular filaments, branched or unbranched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckerMarin2009_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-BeckerMarin2009-28">[28]</a></sup> The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Spirogyra" title="Spirogyra">Spirogyra</a></i> is a filamentous streptophyte alga familiar to many, as it is often used in teaching and is one of the organisms responsible for the algal "scum" on ponds. The freshwater stoneworts strongly resemble land plants and are believed to be their closest relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Growing immersed in fresh water, they consist of a central stalk with whorls of branchlets.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">Fungi</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-One_source plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-one_source" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Question book-new.svg" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>relies largely or entirely upon a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">single source</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Plant##" title="Talk:Plant">talk page</a>. Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plant&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by introducing <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">citations</a> to additional sources.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">Fungi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_collage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1062" data-file-width="1220" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Fungi_collage.jpg/220px-Fungi_collage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Fungi_collage.jpg/330px-Fungi_collage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Fungi_collage.jpg/440px-Fungi_collage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_collage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A variety of <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> species</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus'</a> original classification placed the fungi within the Plantae, since they were unquestionably neither animals or minerals and these were the only other alternatives. With 19th century developments in <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> introduced the new kingdom Protista in addition to Plantae and Animalia, but whether fungi were best placed in the Plantae or should be reclassified as protists remained controversial.  In 1969, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Whittaker_(ecologist)" title="Robert Whittaker (ecologist)">Robert Whittaker</a> proposed the creation of the kingdom Fungi. Molecular evidence has since shown that the <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a> (concestor), of the Fungi was probably more similar to that of the Animalia than to that of Plantae or any other kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whittaker's original reclassification was based on the fundamental difference in nutrition between the Fungi and the Plantae. Unlike plants, which generally gain carbon through photosynthesis, and so are called <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">autotrophs</a>, fungi do not possess chloroplasts and generally obtain carbon by breaking down and absorbing surrounding materials, and so are called <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saprotrophs" title="Saprotrophs">saprotrophs</a>. In addition, the substructure of multicellular fungi is different from that of plants, taking the form of many chitinous microscopic strands called <a href="/wiki/Hypha" title="Hypha">hyphae</a>, which may be further subdivided into cells or may form a <a href="/wiki/Syncytium" title="Syncytium">syncytium</a> containing many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nuclei</a>. Fruiting bodies, of which <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a> are the most familiar example, are the reproductive structures of fungi, and are unlike any structures produced by plants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span></h2>
<p>The table below shows some species count estimates of different green plant (Viridiplantae) divisions. About 85–90% of all plants are flowering plants. Several projects are currently attempting to collect all plant species in online databases, e.g. the <a href="/wiki/World_Flora_Online" title="World Flora Online">World Flora Online</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=World_Plants&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="World Plants (page does not exist)">World Plants</a> both list about 391,000 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WFO_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-WFO-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>The naming of plants is governed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_for_algae,_fungi,_and_plants" title="International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants">International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_for_Cultivated_Plants" title="International Code of Nomenclature for Cultivated Plants">International Code of Nomenclature for Cultivated Plants</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Cultivated_plant_taxonomy" title="Cultivated plant taxonomy">cultivated plant taxonomy</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_plants" title="Evolutionary history of plants">Evolutionary history of plants</a></div>
<p>The evolution of plants has resulted in increasing <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_grade" title="Evolutionary grade">levels of complexity</a>, from the earliest <a href="/wiki/Algal_mat" title="Algal mat">algal mats</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Bryophyte" title="Bryophyte">bryophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycopod" title="Lycopod">lycopods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">ferns</a> to the complex <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperms</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperm" title="Angiosperm">angiosperms</a> of today. Plants in all of these groups continue to thrive, especially in the environments in which they evolved.
</p><p>An algal scum formed on the land <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1,200">1,200</a> million years ago</span>, but it was not until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordovician_Period" title="Ordovician Period">Ordovician Period</a>, around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=450">450</a> million years ago</span>, that land plants appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> However, new evidence from the study of carbon isotope ratios in Precambrian rocks has suggested that complex photosynthetic plants developed on the earth over 1000 m.y.a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> For more than a century it has been assumed that the ancestors of land plants evolved in aquatic environments and then adapted to a life on land, an idea usually credited to botanist <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Orpen_Bower" title="Frederick Orpen Bower">Frederick Orpen Bower</a> in his 1908 book <i>The Origin of a Land Flora</i>. A recent alternative view, supported by genetic evidence, is that they evolved from terrestrial single-celled algae,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and that even the common ancestor of red and green algae, and the unicellular freshwater algae <a href="/wiki/Glaucophyte" title="Glaucophyte">glaucophytes</a>, originated in a terrestrial environment in freshwater biofilms or microbial mats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Primitive land plants began to diversify in the late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silurian_Period" title="Silurian Period">Silurian Period</a>, around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=420">420</a> million years ago</span>, and the results of their diversification are displayed in remarkable detail in an early <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> fossil assemblage from the <a href="/wiki/Rhynie_chert" title="Rhynie chert">Rhynie chert</a>. This chert preserved early plants in cellular detail, petrified in volcanic springs. By the middle of the Devonian Period most of the features recognised in plants today are present, including roots, leaves and secondary wood, and by late Devonian times seeds had evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothwelletal989_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rothwelletal989-49">[49]</a></sup> Late Devonian plants had thereby reached a degree of sophistication that allowed them to form forests of tall trees. Evolutionary innovation continued in the Carboniferous and later geological periods and is ongoing today. Most plant groups were relatively unscathed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permo-Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permo-Triassic extinction event">Permo-Triassic extinction event</a>, although the structures of communities changed. This may have set the scene for the evolution of flowering plants in the Triassic (~<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=200">200</a> million years ago</span>), which exploded in the Cretaceous and Tertiary. The latest major group of plants to evolve were the grasses, which became important in the mid Tertiary, from around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=40">40</a> million years ago</span>. The grasses, as well as many other groups, evolved new mechanisms of metabolism to survive the low CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> and warm, dry conditions of the tropics over the last <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=10">10</a> million years</span>.
</p><p>A 1997 proposed <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a> of Plantae, after Kenrick and Crane,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> is as follows, with modification to the Pteridophyta from Smith <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prasinophyceae" title="Prasinophyceae">Prasinophyceae</a> are a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> assemblage of early diverging green algal lineages, but are treated as a group outside the Chlorophyta:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leliaert_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-leliaert-52">[52]</a></sup> later authors have not followed this suggestion.
</p>

<p>A newer proposed classification follows Leliaert et al. 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeliaertVerbruggen2011_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeliaertVerbruggen2011-53">[53]</a></sup> and modified with Silar 2016<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sánchez-Baracaldo_E7737–E7745_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sánchez-Baracaldo_E7737–E7745-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gitzendanner_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gitzendanner-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-55">[55]</a></sup> for the green algae clades and Novíkov &amp; Barabaš-Krasni 2015<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> for the land plants clade. Notice that the Prasinophyceae are here placed inside the Chlorophyta.
</p>

<p>Later, a phylogeny based on genomes and transcriptomes from 1,153 plant species was proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1000plants_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-1000plants-57">[57]</a></sup> The placing of algal groups is supported by phylogenies based on genomes from the Mesostigmatophyceae and Chlorokybophyceae that have since been sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MvirideGenome1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-MvirideGenome1-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mv_and_Ca_Genomes_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mv_and_Ca_Genomes-59">[59]</a></sup> The classification of Bryophyta is supported both by Puttick <i>et al.</i> 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puttick2018_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Puttick2018-60">[60]</a></sup> and by phylogenies involving the hornwort genomes that have also since been sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HornwortGenome1_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-HornwortGenome1-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HornwortGenome2_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-HornwortGenome2-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Embryophytes">Embryophytes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Embryophyte" title="Embryophyte">Embryophyte</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plant&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ferns02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1484" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ferns02.jpg/220px-Ferns02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ferns02.jpg/330px-Ferns02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ferns02.jpg/440px-Ferns02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ferns02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Dicksonia_antarctica" title="Dicksonia antarctica">Dicksonia antarctica</a></i>, a species of <a href="/wiki/Tree_fern" title="Tree fern">tree fern</a></div></div></div><p>The plants that are likely most familiar to us are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> land plants, called <a href="/wiki/Embryophyte" title="Embryophyte">embryophytes</a>. Embryophytes include the <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a>, such as ferns, conifers and flowering plants. They also include the <i><a href="/wiki/Bryophyte" title="Bryophyte">bryophytes</a></i>, of which <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marchantiophyta" title="Marchantiophyta">liverworts</a> are the most common.
</p><p>All of these plants have <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> cells with <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a> composed of <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, and most obtain their energy through <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>, water and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> to synthesize food. About three hundred plant species do not photosynthesize but are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> on other species of photosynthetic plants. Embryophytes are distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a>, which represent a mode of photosynthetic life similar to the kind modern plants are believed to have evolved from, by having specialized reproductive organs protected by non-reproductive tissues.
</p><p>Bryophytes first appeared during the early <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a>. They mainly live in habitats where moisture is available for significant periods, although some species, such as <a href="/wiki/Targionia_(plant)" title="Targionia (plant)"><i>Targionia</i></a>, are desiccation-tolerant. Most species of bryophytes remain small throughout their life-cycle. This involves an alternation between two generations: a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> stage, called the <a href="/wiki/Gametophyte" title="Gametophyte">gametophyte</a>, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> stage, called the <a href="/wiki/Sporophyte" title="Sporophyte">sporophyte</a>. In bryophytes, the sporophyte is always unbranched and remains nutritionally dependent on its parent gametophyte. The embryophytes have the ability to secrete a <a href="/wiki/Plant_cuticle" title="Plant cuticle">cuticle</a> on their outer surface, a waxy layer that confers resistance to desiccation. In the <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hornwort" title="Hornwort">hornworts</a> a cuticle is usually only produced on the sporophyte. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">Stomata</a> are absent from liverworts, but occur on the sporangia of mosses and hornworts, allowing gas exchange.
</p><p>Vascular plants first appeared during the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> period, and by the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> had diversified and spread into many different terrestrial environments. They developed a number of adaptations that allowed them to spread into increasingly more arid places, notably the vascular tissues <a href="/wiki/Xylem" title="Xylem">xylem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phloem" title="Phloem">phloem</a>, that transport water and food throughout the organism. Root systems capable of obtaining soil water and nutrients also evolved during the Devonian. In modern vascular plants, the sporophyte is typically large, branched, nutritionally independent and long-lived, but there is increasing evidence that Paleozoic gametophytes were just as complex as the sporophytes. The gametophytes of all vascular plant groups evolved to become reduced in size and prominence in the life cycle.
</p><p>In seed plants, the <a href="/wiki/Gametophyte#Heteromorphy" title="Gametophyte">microgametophyte</a> is reduced from a multicellular free-living organism to a few cells in a pollen grain and the miniaturised <a href="/wiki/Gametophyte#Heteromorphy" title="Gametophyte">megagametophyte</a> remains inside the megasporangium, attached to and dependent on the parent plant. A megasporangium enclosed in a protective layer called an integument is known as an <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">ovule</a>. After fertilisation by means of sperm produced by <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> grains, an embryo sporophyte develops inside the ovule. The integument becomes a seed coat, and the ovule develops into a seed. Seed plants can survive and reproduce in extremely arid conditions, because they are not dependent on free water for the movement of sperm, or the development of free living gametophytes.
</p><p>The first seed plants, <a href="/wiki/Pteridospermatophyta" title="Pteridospermatophyta">pteridosperms</a> (seed ferns), now extinct, appeared in the Devonian and diversified through the Carboniferous. They were the ancestors of modern <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperms</a>, of which four surviving groups are widespread today, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a>, which are dominant <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a> in several <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a>. The name gymnosperm comes from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γυμνόσπερμος</span></span>, a composite of <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γυμνός</span></span> (<span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">gymnos</i></span> <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'naked') and <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">σπέρμα</span></span> (<span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">sperma</i></span> <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'seed'), as the ovules and subsequent seeds are not enclosed in a protective structure (carpels or fruit), but are borne naked, typically on cone scales.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossils">Fossils</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Paleobotany" title="Paleobotany">Paleobotany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_plants" title="Evolutionary history of plants">Evolutionary history of plants</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1231" data-file-width="821" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg/170px-Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg/255px-Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg/340px-Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A petrified log in <a href="/wiki/Petrified_Forest_National_Park" title="Petrified Forest National Park">Petrified Forest National Park</a>, Arizona</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plant&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Plant <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> include roots, wood, leaves, seeds, fruit, <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">phytoliths</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> (the fossilized resin produced by some plants). Fossil land plants are recorded in terrestrial, lacustrine, fluvial and nearshore marine sediments. <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">Pollen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spores" title="Spores">spores</a> and algae (<a href="/wiki/Dinoflagellate" title="Dinoflagellate">dinoflagellates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acritarch" title="Acritarch">acritarchs</a>) are used for dating sedimentary rock sequences. The remains of fossil plants are not as common as fossil animals, although plant fossils are locally abundant in many regions worldwide.
</p><p>The earliest fossils clearly assignable to Kingdom Plantae are fossil green algae from the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a>. These fossils resemble <a href="/wiki/Calcification" title="Calcification">calcified</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> members of the <a href="/wiki/Dasycladales" title="Dasycladales">Dasycladales</a>. Earlier <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> fossils are known that resemble single-cell green algae, but definitive identity with that group of algae is uncertain.
</p><p>The earliest fossils attributed to green algae date from the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> (ca. 1200 mya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The resistant outer walls of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prasinophyceae" title="Prasinophyceae">prasinophyte</a> cysts (known as phycomata) are well preserved in fossil deposits of the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> (ca. 250–540 mya). A filamentous fossil (<i>Proterocladus</i>) from middle Neoproterozoic deposits (ca. 750 mya) has been attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Cladophorales" title="Cladophorales">Cladophorales</a>, while the oldest reliable records of the <a href="/wiki/Bryopsidales" title="Bryopsidales">Bryopsidales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dasycladales" title="Dasycladales">Dasycladales</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Charales" title="Charales">stoneworts</a> are from the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leliaert_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-leliaert-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ButterfieldKnoll1994_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ButterfieldKnoll1994-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest known fossils of embryophytes date from the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a>, though such fossils are fragmentary. By the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a>, fossils of whole plants are preserved, including the simple vascular plant <i><a href="/wiki/Cooksonia" title="Cooksonia">Cooksonia</a></i> in mid-Silurian and the much larger and more complex <a href="/wiki/Lycophyte" title="Lycophyte">lycophyte</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baragwanathia_longifolia" title="Baragwanathia longifolia">Baragwanathia longifolia</a></i> in late Silurian. From the early Devonian <a href="/wiki/Rhynie_chert" title="Rhynie chert">Rhynie chert</a>, detailed fossils of lycophytes and <a href="/wiki/Rhyniophyte" title="Rhyniophyte">rhyniophytes</a> have been found that show details of the individual cells within the plant organs and the symbiotic association of these plants with fungi of the order <a href="/wiki/Glomerales" title="Glomerales">Glomales</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devonian_period" title="Devonian period">Devonian period</a> also saw the evolution of leaves and roots, and the first modern tree, <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteris" title="Archaeopteris">Archaeopteris</a></i>. This tree with fern-like foliage and a trunk with conifer-like wood was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterosporous" title="Heterosporous">heterosporous</a> producing spores of two different sizes, an early step in the evolution of seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_measure" title="Coal measure">Coal measures</a> are a major source of <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> plant fossils, with many groups of plants in existence at this time. The spoil heaps of coal mines are the best places to collect; <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> itself is the remains of fossilised plants, though structural detail of the plant fossils is rarely visible in coal. In the <a href="/wiki/Fossil_Grove" title="Fossil Grove">Fossil Grove</a> at <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Park,_Glasgow" title="Victoria Park, Glasgow">Victoria Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, Scotland, the stumps of <i><a href="/wiki/Lepidodendron" title="Lepidodendron">Lepidodendron</a></i> trees are found in their original growth positions.
</p><p>The fossilized remains of <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifer</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperm" title="Angiosperm">angiosperm</a> <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">stems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Branch" title="Branch">branches</a> may be locally abundant in lake and inshore <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> eras. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coast_Redwood" title="Coast Redwood">Sequoia</a> and its allies, <a href="/wiki/Magnolia" title="Magnolia">magnolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arecaceae" title="Arecaceae">palms</a> are often found.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="389" data-file-width="633" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg/220px-Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg/330px-Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg/440px-Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pangaea_Glossopteris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Range of <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">pangaea</a> glossopteris.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Petrified_wood" title="Petrified wood">Petrified wood</a> is common in some parts of the world, and is most frequently found in arid or desert areas where it is more readily exposed by <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>. Petrified wood is often heavily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicified" title="Silicified">silicified</a> (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic material</a> replaced by <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a>), and the impregnated tissue is often preserved in fine detail. Such specimens may be cut and polished using <a href="/wiki/Lapidary" title="Lapidary">lapidary</a> equipment. Fossil forests of petrified wood have been found in all continents.
</p><p>Fossils of seed ferns such as <i><a href="/wiki/Glossopteris" title="Glossopteris">Glossopteris</a></i> are widely distributed throughout several continents of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>, a fact that gave support to <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Wegener" title="Alfred Wegener">Alfred Wegener</a>'s early ideas regarding <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">Continental drift</a> theory.
</p>
<h2><span id="Structure.2C_growth.2C_and_development"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure,_growth,_and_development">Structure, growth, and development</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Plant_morphology" title="Plant morphology">Plant morphology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leaf_1_web.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Leaf_1_web.jpg/220px-Leaf_1_web.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Leaf_1_web.jpg/330px-Leaf_1_web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Leaf_1_web.jpg/440px-Leaf_1_web.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leaf_1_web.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaf</a> is usually the primary site of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> in plants.</div></div></div>
<p>Most of the solid material in a plant is taken from the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. Through the process of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, most plants use the energy in <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> to convert <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the atmosphere, plus <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, into simple <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a>. These sugars are then used as building blocks and form the main structural component of the plant. <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">Chlorophyll</a>, a green-colored, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>-containing <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> is essential to this process; it is generally present in plant <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a>, and often in other plant parts as well. <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_plant" title="Parasitic plant">Parasitic plants</a>, on the other hand, use the resources of their host to provide the materials needed for metabolism and growth.
</p><p>Plants usually rely on soil primarily for support and water (in quantitative terms), but they also obtain <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, magnesium and other elemental <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> from the soil. <a href="/wiki/Epiphyte" title="Epiphyte">Epiphytic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithophyte" title="Lithophyte">lithophytic</a> plants depend on air and nearby debris for nutrients, and <a href="/wiki/Carnivorous_plant" title="Carnivorous plant">carnivorous plants</a> supplement their nutrient requirements, particularly for nitrogen and phosphorus, with insect prey that they capture. For the majority of plants to grow successfully they also require <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> in the atmosphere and around their roots (<a href="/wiki/Soil_gas" title="Soil gas">soil gas</a>) for <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a>. Plants use oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> (which may be produced from stored <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>) to provide energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Some plants grow as submerged aquatics, using oxygen dissolved in the surrounding water, and a few specialized vascular plants, such as <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangroves</a> and reed (<i><a href="/wiki/Phragmites_australis" title="Phragmites australis">Phragmites australis</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crawford_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crawford-68">[68]</a></sup> can grow with their roots in <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">anoxic</a> conditions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_affecting_growth">Factors affecting growth</span></h3>
<p>The genome of a plant controls its growth. For example, selected varieties or genotypes of wheat grow rapidly, maturing within 110 days, whereas others, in the same environmental conditions, grow more slowly and mature within 155 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Growth is also determined by environmental factors, such as <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, available <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, available <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and available <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> in the soil. Any change in the availability of these external conditions will be reflected in the plant's growth and the timing of its development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Biotic factors also affect plant growth. Plants can be so crowded that no single individual produces normal growth, causing <a href="/wiki/Etiolation" title="Etiolation">etiolation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorosis" title="Chlorosis">chlorosis</a>. Optimal plant growth can be hampered by grazing animals, suboptimal soil composition, lack of <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhiza" title="Mycorrhiza">mycorrhizal</a> fungi, and attacks by insects or <a href="/wiki/Plant_pathology" title="Plant pathology">plant diseases</a>, including those caused by bacteria, fungi, viruses, and nematodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5096" data-file-width="5096" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg/220px-Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg/330px-Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg/440px-Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eenbruinigherfstblad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>There is no photosynthesis in deciduous leaves in autumn.</div></div></div>
<p>Simple plants like algae may have short life spans as individuals, but their populations are commonly seasonal. <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">Annual plants</a> grow and reproduce within one <a href="/wiki/Growing_season" title="Growing season">growing season</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biennial_plant" title="Biennial plant">biennial plants</a> grow for two growing seasons and usually reproduce in second year, and <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial plants</a> live for many growing seasons and once mature will often reproduce annually. These designations often depend on climate and other environmental factors. Plants that are annual in <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> regions can be biennial or perennial in warmer climates. Among the vascular plants, perennials include both <a href="/wiki/Evergreen" title="Evergreen">evergreens</a> that keep their leaves the entire year, and <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> plants that lose their leaves for some part of it. In temperate and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreal_climate" title="Boreal climate">boreal climates</a>, they generally lose their leaves during the winter; many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">tropical</a> plants lose their leaves during the <a href="/wiki/Dry_season" title="Dry season">dry season</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The growth rate of plants is extremely variable. Some mosses grow less than 0.001 millimeters per hour (mm/h), while most trees grow 0.025–0.250 mm/h. Some climbing species, such as <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">kudzu</a>, which do not need to produce thick supportive tissue, may grow up to 12.5 mm/h.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Plants protect themselves from <a href="/wiki/Frost" title="Frost">frost</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> stress with <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze_protein" title="Antifreeze protein">antifreeze proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_shock_protein" title="Heat shock protein">heat-shock proteins</a> and sugars (<a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> is common). LEA (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Embryogenesis_Abundant_proteins" title="Late Embryogenesis Abundant proteins">Late Embryogenesis Abundant</a>) protein expression is induced by stresses and protects other proteins from aggregation as a result of <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freezing" title="Freezing">freezing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_freezing">Effects of freezing</span></h4>
<p>When water freezes in plants, the consequences for the plant depend very much on whether the freezing occurs within cells (intracellularly) or outside cells in intercellular spaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glerum1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-glerum1-71">[71]</a></sup> Intracellular freezing, which usually kills the cell<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lyons_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-lyons-72">[72]</a></sup> regardless of the hardiness of the plant and its tissues, seldom occurs in nature because rates of cooling are rarely high enough to support it. Rates of cooling of several degrees Celsius per minute are typically needed to cause intracellular formation of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazur_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-mazur-73">[73]</a></sup> At rates of cooling of a few degrees Celsius per hour, segregation of ice occurs in intercellular spaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sakai4_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-sakai4-74">[74]</a></sup> This may or may not be lethal, depending on the hardiness of the tissue. At freezing temperatures, water in the intercellular spaces of plant tissue freezes first, though the water may remain unfrozen until temperatures drop below −7 °C (19 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glerum1_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-glerum1-71">[71]</a></sup> After the initial formation of intercellular ice, the cells shrink as water is lost to the segregated ice, and the cells undergo freeze-drying. This dehydration is now considered the fundamental cause of freezing injury.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_damage_and_repair">DNA damage and repair</span></h3>
<p>Plants are continuously exposed to a range of biotic and abiotic stresses.  These stresses often cause <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damage</a> directly, or indirectly via the generation of <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18707020_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18707020-75">[75]</a></sup> Plants are capable of a DNA damage response that is a critical mechanism for maintaining genome stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26617076_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26617076-76">[76]</a></sup> The DNA damage response is particularly important during <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seed</a> <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>, since seed quality tends to deteriorate with age in association with DNA damage accumulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25750428_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25750428-77">[77]</a></sup> During germination repair processes are activated to deal with this accumulated DNA damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11321247_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11321247-78">[78]</a></sup> In particular, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-stranded_break" title="Single-stranded break">single-</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double-stranded_break" title="Double-stranded break">double</a>-strand breaks in DNA can be <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">repaired</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20584150_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20584150-79">[79]</a></sup> Double-strand repair in plants often produce DNA junctions with <a href="/wiki/Genetic_recombination" title="Genetic recombination">structural alterations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britt-1996_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britt-1996-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 89">: 89 </span></sup> The DNA checkpoint kinase <a href="/wiki/ATM_serine/threonine_kinase" title="ATM serine/threonine kinase">ATM</a> has a key role in integrating progression through germination with repair responses to the DNA damages accumulated by the aged seed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27503884_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27503884-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_cells">Plant cells</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plant_cell_structure-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="649" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Plant_cell_structure-en.svg/220px-Plant_cell_structure-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Plant_cell_structure-en.svg/330px-Plant_cell_structure-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Plant_cell_structure-en.svg/440px-Plant_cell_structure-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plant_cell_structure-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plant cell structure</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-One_source plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-one_source" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Question book-new.svg" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>relies largely or entirely upon a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">single source</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Plant##" title="Talk:Plant">talk page</a>. Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plant&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by introducing <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">citations</a> to additional sources.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plant_cell" title="Plant cell">Plant cell</a></div>
<p>Plant cells are typically distinguished by their large water-filled central <a href="/wiki/Vacuole" title="Vacuole">vacuole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a>, and rigid <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a> that are made up of <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemicellulose" title="Hemicellulose">hemicellulose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">Cell division</a> is also characterized by the development of a <a href="/wiki/Phragmoplast" title="Phragmoplast">phragmoplast</a> for the construction of a <a href="/wiki/Cell_plate" title="Cell plate">cell plate</a> in the late stages of <a href="/wiki/Cytokinesis" title="Cytokinesis">cytokinesis</a>. Just as in animals, plant cells differentiate and develop into multiple cell types. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totipotent" title="Totipotent">Totipotent</a> <a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristematic</a> cells can differentiate into <a href="/wiki/Vascular_tissue" title="Vascular tissue">vascular</a>, storage, protective (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(botany)" title="Epidermis (botany)">epidermal layer</a>), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_sexuality" title="Plant sexuality">reproductive</a> tissues, with more primitive plants lacking some tissue types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plant_physiology" title="Plant physiology">Plant physiology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photosynthesis">Photosynthesis</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plant&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">Photosynthesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biological_pigment" title="Biological pigment">Biological pigment</a></div>
<p>Plants <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a>, which means that they manufacture their own food molecules using energy obtained from <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>. The primary mechanism plants have for capturing light energy is the <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a>. All green plants contain two forms of chlorophyll, <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll_a" title="Chlorophyll a">chlorophyll <i>a</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll_b" title="Chlorophyll b">chlorophyll <i>b</i></a>. The latter of these pigments is not found in red or brown algae.
The simple equation of photosynthesis is as follows:
</p>
